<p>A <b>ship</b> is a large <a href="/wiki/Watercraft" title="Watercraft">watercraft</a> that travels the world's oceans and other sufficiently deep <a href="/wiki/Sea_lane" title="Sea lane">waterways</a>, carrying cargo or passengers, or in support of specialized missions, such as defense, research, and fishing. Ships are generally distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">boats</a>, based on size, shape, load capacity, and purpose. Ships have supported <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">warfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">colonization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>. After the 15th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">new crops</a> that had come from and to the Americas via the European seafarers significantly contributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_population_growth" title="World population growth">world population growth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wikiwix_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wikiwix-1">[1]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_transport" title="Ship transport">Ship transport</a> is responsible for the largest portion of world commerce.
</p><p>The word <i>ship</i> has meant, depending on the era and the context, either just a large vessel or specifically a <a href="/wiki/Full-rigged_ship" title="Full-rigged ship">ship-rigged</a> <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ship" title="Sailing ship">sailing ship</a> with three or more masts, each of which is <a href="/wiki/Square_rig" title="Square rig">square-rigged</a>.
</p><p>As of 2016, there were more than 49,000 <a href="/wiki/Merchant_ship" title="Merchant ship">merchant ships</a>, totaling almost 1.8 billion <a href="/wiki/Deadweight_tonnage" title="Deadweight tonnage">dead weight tons</a>. Of these 28% were <a href="/wiki/Oil_tanker" title="Oil tanker">oil tankers</a>, 43% were <a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">bulk carriers</a>, and 13% were <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNCTAD_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNCTAD-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(A-L)" title="Glossary of nautical terms (A-L)">Glossary of nautical terms (A-L)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)</a></div><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ship_diagram-numbers.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="680" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ship_diagram-numbers.svg/310px-Ship_diagram-numbers.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ship_diagram-numbers.svg/465px-Ship_diagram-numbers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Ship_diagram-numbers.svg/620px-Ship_diagram-numbers.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ship_diagram-numbers.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main parts of ship. <b>1</b>:Â  <a href="/wiki/Funnel_(ship)" title="Funnel (ship)">Funnel</a>; <b>2</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">Stern</a>; <b>3</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">Propeller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">Rudder</a>; <b>4</b>:Â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portside" title="Portside">Portside</a> (the right side is known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starboard" title="Starboard">starboard</a>); <b>5</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">Anchor</a>; <b>6</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Bulbous_bow" title="Bulbous bow">Bulbous bow</a>; <b>7</b>:Â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">Bow</a>; <b>8</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">Deck</a>; <b>9</b>:Â <a href="/wiki/Superstructure" title="Superstructure">Superstructure</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ships are typically larger than boats, but there is no universally accepted distinction between the two. Ships generally can remain at sea for longer periods of time than boats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> A legal definition of ship from <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> <a href="/wiki/Case_law" title="Case law">case law</a> is a vessel that carries goods by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclo_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclo-4">[4]</a></sup> A common notion is that a ship can carry a boat, but not <i>vice versa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-5">[5]</a></sup> A ship is likely to have a full-time crew assigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> A US Navy rule of thumb is that ships heel towards the <i>outside</i> of a sharp turn, whereas boats heel towards the <i>inside</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonsson_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonsson-7">[7]</a></sup> because of the relative location of the <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> versus the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_of_buoyancy" title="Center of buoyancy">center of buoyancy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ridley_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ridley-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Faltinsen_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Faltinsen-9">[9]</a></sup> American and British 19th century maritime law distinguished "vessels" from other watercraft; ships and boats fall in one legal category, whereas open boats and rafts are not considered vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>, the word <i>ship</i> might apply generally to a seagoing vessel or particularly to a full-rigged sailing ship with three or more <a href="/wiki/Mast_(sailing)" title="Mast (sailing)">masts</a>, all square-rigged.  Other rigs on seagoing vessels included <a href="/wiki/Brig" title="Brig">brig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barque" title="Barque">barque</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Barquentine" title="Barquentine">barquentine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett_2005_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett_2005-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">:â8â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Underhill_1946_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Underhill_1946-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">:â2â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer_1975_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer_1975-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 222">:â222â</span></sup>
</p><p>Some large vessels are traditionally called <i>boats</i>, notably <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>  Others include <a href="/wiki/Lake_freighter" title="Lake freighter">Great Lakes freighters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riverboat" title="Riverboat">riverboats</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferryboat" title="Ferryboat">ferryboats</a>, which may be designed for operation on inland or protected coastal waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In most maritime traditions ships have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_naming_and_launching" title="Ship naming and launching">individual names</a>, and modern ships may belong to a <a href="/wiki/Ship_class" title="Ship class">ship class</a> often named after its first ship.
</p><p>In many documents the ship name is introduced with a <a href="/wiki/Ship_prefix" title="Ship prefix">ship prefix</a> being an abbreviation of the ship class, for example "MS" (motor ship) or "SV" (sailing vessel), making it easier to distinguish a ship name from other individual names in a text.
</p><p>"Ship" (along with "nation") is an English word that has retained a female <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">grammatical gender</a> in some usages, which allows it sometimes to be referred to as a "she" without being of female <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">natural gender</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history" title="Maritime history">Maritime history</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sailing_ship" title="Sailing ship">Sailing ship</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_antiquity">Prehistory and antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory and antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_developments">Asian developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Asian developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1840" data-file-width="2344" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg/220px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg/330px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg/440px-Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlas_pittoresque_pl_096.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fiji#Early_settlement" title="Fiji">Fijian</a> voyaging <a href="/wiki/Outrigger_boat" title="Outrigger boat">outrigger boat</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Crab_claw_sail" title="Crab claw sail">crab claw sail</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_ship.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="1183" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/220px-Borobudur_ship.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/330px-Borobudur_ship.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Borobudur_ship.JPG/440px-Borobudur_ship.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borobudur_ship.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>One of the sailing trimarans depicted in <a href="/wiki/Borobudur" title="Borobudur">Borobudur</a> temple, c.Â 8th century AD in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest attestations of ships in <a href="/wiki/Maritime_transport" title="Maritime transport">maritime transport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_ship" title="Model ship">model ships</a>, which date back to the 4th millennium BC. In archaic texts in <a href="/wiki/Uruk" title="Uruk">Uruk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, the ideogram for "ship" is attested, but in the inscriptions of the kings of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, ships were first mentioned in connection to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_trade" title="Maritime trade">maritime trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare" title="Naval warfare">naval warfare</a> at around 2500â2350 BCE.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="These clay models are surely boats. The interpretation of texts needs an RS. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> originated in what is now <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>. From here, they took part in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_Expansion" title="Austronesian Expansion">Austronesian Expansion</a>. Their distinctive maritime technology was integral to this movement and included <a href="/wiki/Catamaran" title="Catamaran">catamarans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outrigger_ship" title="Outrigger ship">outriggers</a>. It has been deduced that they had sails some time before 2000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horridge_2006_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horridge_2006-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 144">:â144â</span></sup> Their <a href="/wiki/Crab_claw_sail" title="Crab claw sail">crab claw sails</a> enabled them to sail for vast distances in open ocean.  From Taiwan, they rapidly colonized the islands of <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">Maritime Southeast Asia</a>, then sailed further onwards to <a href="/wiki/Micronesia" title="Micronesia">Micronesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Island_Melanesia" title="Island Melanesia">Island Melanesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, eventually colonizing a territory spanning half the globe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1974_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1974-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Austronesian rigs were distinctive in that they had spars supporting both the upper and lower edges of the sails (and sometimes in between).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1974_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1974-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-18">[18]</a></sup> The sails were also made from woven leaves, usually from <a href="/wiki/Pandanus" title="Pandanus">pandan</a> plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirch2012_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirch2012-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallaher2014_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallaher2014-20">[20]</a></sup> These were complemented by paddlers, who usually positioned themselves on platforms on the <a href="/wiki/Outrigger" title="Outrigger">outriggers</a> in the larger boats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1974_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1974-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1981_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1981-21">[21]</a></sup> Austronesian ships ranged in complexity from simple <a href="/wiki/Dugout_canoe" title="Dugout canoe">dugout canoes</a> with outriggers or lashed together to large edge-pegged plank-built boats built around a keel made from a dugout canoe. Their designs were unique, evolving from ancient rafts to the characteristic double-hulled, single-outrigger, and double-outrigger designs of Austronesian ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doran1981_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Doran1981-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early Austronesian sailors influenced the development of sailing technologies in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">Southern India</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" title="Austronesian maritime trade network">Austronesian maritime trade network</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, the precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> route and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_silk_road" title="Maritime silk road">maritime silk road</a>, which was established at around 1500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellina_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellina-22">[22]</a></sup> Some scholars believe that the triangular Austronesian crab claw sail may have influenced the development of the <a href="/wiki/Lateen" title="Lateen">lateen</a> sail in western ships due to early contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mahdi1999_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mahdi1999-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Junk_rig" title="Junk rig">junk rigs</a> of Chinese ships is also believed to be originally <a href="/wiki/Javanese_people" title="Javanese people">Javanese</a> in origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnstone_1980_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnstone_1980-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hourani_1951_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hourani_1951-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1st century AD, people from the <a href="/wiki/Nusantara_(archipelago)" title="Nusantara (archipelago)">Indonesian archipelago</a> already made large ships measuring over 50 m long and standing 4â7 m out of the water. They could carry 600â1000 people and 250â1000 ton cargo. These ships were known as <i>kunlun bo</i> or <i>k'unlun po</i> (å´å´è¶, lit. "ship of the <a href="/wiki/Kunlun_(mythology)" title="Kunlun (mythology)">Kunlun</a> people") by the Chinese, and <i>kolandiaphonta</i> by the Greeks. They had 4â7 masts and were able to sail against the wind due to the usage of <a href="/wiki/Tanja_sail" title="Tanja sail">tanja sails</a>. These ships reached as far as <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 262">:â262â</span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:112_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-:112-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 347">:â347â</span></sup>
</p><p>In China, miniature models of ships that feature steering oars have been dated to the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> (c.Â 475â221 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tom_1989_103_104_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-tom_1989_103_104-28">[28]</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, a well kept naval fleet was an integral part of the military. Sternpost-mounted rudders started to appear on Chinese ship models starting in the 1st century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tom_1989_103_104_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-tom_1989_103_104-28">[28]</a></sup> However, these early Chinese ships were fluvial (riverine), and were not seaworthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pham_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pham-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The Chinese only acquired sea-going ship technologies in the 10th century AD <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a> after contact with Southeast Asian <a href="/wiki/Djong_(ship)" title="Djong (ship)">djong</a> trading ships, leading to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnstone_1980_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnstone_1980-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hourani_1951_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hourani_1951-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mediterranean_developments">Mediterranean developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mediterranean developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1197" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg/220px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg/330px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg/440px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Menna_013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egyptian sailing ship, c.Â 1422â1411 BC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoenician_ship.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Phoenician_ship.jpg/220px-Phoenician_ship.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Phoenician_ship.jpg/330px-Phoenician_ship.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Phoenician_ship.jpg/440px-Phoenician_ship.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phoenician_ship.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> ship carved on the face of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ship_sarcophagus" title="Ship sarcophagus">"Ship Sarcophagus"</a>, c.Â 2nd century AD</div></div></div>
<p>Archaeological evidence in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, dated to 3000 BC, shows wooden <a href="/wiki/Plank_(wood)" title="Plank (wood)">planks</a> assembled into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hull_(ship)" title="Hull (ship)">hull</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIA-31">[31]</a></sup>  They used woven <a href="/wiki/Strap" title="Strap">straps</a> to lash the planks together,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIA-31">[31]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cyperus_papyrus" title="Cyperus papyrus">reeds</a> or grass stuffed between the planks helped to seal the seams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIA_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-AIA-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[note 1]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geographer" title="Geographer">geographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Agatharchides" title="Agatharchides">Agatharchides</a> had documented ship-faring among the early <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptians</a>:  <i>"During the prosperous period of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">Old Kingdom</a>, between the <a href="/wiki/30th_century_BC" title="30th century BC">30th</a> and <a href="/wiki/25th_century_BC" title="25th century BC">25th centuries BC</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">river</a>-routes were kept in order, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> ships sailed the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> as far as the <a href="/wiki/Myrrh" title="Myrrh">myrrh</a>-country."</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sneferu" title="Sneferu">Sneferu</a>'s ancient cedar wood ship <a href="/wiki/Praise_of_the_Two_Lands_(ship)" title="Praise of the Two Lands (ship)">Praise of the Two Lands</a> is the first reference recorded (2613 BC) to a ship being referred to by name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a> were perfectly at ease building sailboats. A remarkable example of their <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> skills was the <a href="/wiki/Khufu_ship" title="Khufu ship">Khufu ship</a>, a vessel 143 feet (44Â m) in length entombed at the foot of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Great Pyramid of Giza</a> around 2500 BC and found intact in 1954.
</p><p>The oldest discovered sea faring hulled boat is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age" title="Late Bronze Age">Late Bronze Age</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uluburun" title="Uluburun">Uluburun</a> shipwreck off the coast of Turkey, dating back to 1300 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1200 B.C., the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> were building large merchant ships. In world maritime history, declares Richard Woodman, they are recognized as "the first true seafarers, founding the art of pilotage, cabotage, and navigation" and the architects of "the first true ship, built of planks, capable of carrying a deadweight cargo and being sailed and steered."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="14th_through_the_18th_centuries">14th through the 18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 14th through the 18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2019</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asian_developments_2">Asian developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Asian developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4><p>
At this time, ships were developing in Asia in much the same way as Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2019)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> Japan used defensive naval techniques in the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a> in 1281. It is likely that the Mongols of the time took advantage of both European and Asian shipbuilding techniques.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2019)">according to whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> During the 15th century, China's <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> assembled one of the largest and most powerful naval fleets in the world for the <a href="/wiki/Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Ming treasure voyages">diplomatic and power projection voyages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>. Elsewhere in Japan in the 15th century, one of the world's first iron-clads, "TekkÅsen" (<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E9%89%84%E7%94%B2%E8%88%B9" title="ja:éç²è¹">éç²è¹</a>), literally meaning "iron ships",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> was also developed. In Japan, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sengoku_era" title="Sengoku era">Sengoku era</a> from the 15th century to 17th century, the great struggle for feudal supremacy was fought, in part, by coastal fleets of several hundred boats, including the <a href="/wiki/Atakebune" title="Atakebune">atakebune</a>. In Korea, in the early 15th century during the <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> era, "<a href="/wiki/Turtle_ship" title="Turtle ship">Geobukseon</a>"(ê±°ë¶ì ), was developed. The "turtle ship", as it was called is recognized as the first armored ship in the world.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="869" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png/220px-Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png/330px-Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png/440px-Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japanese-Tokugawa-Ship-Ataka-Maru.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Japanese <a href="/wiki/Atakebune" title="Atakebune">atakebune</a> from the 16th century</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="European_developments">European developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: European developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nao_Victoria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/220px-Nao_Victoria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/330px-Nao_Victoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Nao_Victoria.jpg/440px-Nao_Victoria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nao_Victoria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of Magellan's <i><a href="/wiki/Victoria_(ship)" title="Victoria (ship)">Victoria</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Ferdinand Magellan</a> led the first expedition that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumnavigated" title="Circumnavigated">circumnavigated</a> the globe in 1519â1522.</div></div></div>
<p>Several civilizations became sea powers. Such examples include the maritime republics of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_navy" title="Byzantine navy">Byzantine navy</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> used their <a href="/wiki/Knarr" title="Knarr">knarrs</a> to explore <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, trade in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> and plunder many of the coastal regions of Western Europe.
</p><p>Towards the end of the 14th century, ships like the <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">carrack</a> began to develop towers on the bow and stern. These towers decreased the vessel's stability, and in the 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a>, designed by the <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a>, based on the Arabic <i>qarib</i> which could sail closer to the wind, became more widely used. The towers were gradually replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Forecastle" title="Forecastle">forecastle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">sterncastle</a>, as in the carrack <a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa MarÃ­a (ship)"><i>Santa MarÃ­a</i></a> of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a>. This increased <a href="/wiki/Freeboard_(nautical)" title="Freeboard (nautical)">freeboard</a> allowed another innovation: the <a href="/wiki/Scupper" title="Scupper">freeing port</a>, and the artillery associated with it.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">carrack</a> and then the <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">caravel</a> were developed in <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>. After Columbus, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">European exploration</a> rapidly accelerated, and many new trade routes were established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In 1498, by reaching India, <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a> proved that access to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> was possible. These explorations in the Atlantic and Indian Oceans were soon followed by <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, who explored the Portuguese and Spanish trade routes into the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, reaching <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in 1606 and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> in 1642.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Love-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialization_and_modernization">Specialization and modernization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Specialization and modernization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">December 2019</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/220px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/330px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg/440px-Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trafalgar-Auguste_Mayer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trafalgar" title="Battle of Trafalgar">Battle of Trafalgar</a> by <a href="/wiki/Auguste_%C3%89tienne_Fran%C3%A7ois_Mayer" title="Auguste Ãtienne FranÃ§ois Mayer">Auguste Mayer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="1708" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg/220px-Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg/330px-Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg/440px-Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amerigo_vespucci_1976_nyc_aufgetakelt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italian</a> <a href="/wiki/Full-rigged_ship" title="Full-rigged ship">full-rigged ship</a> <a href="/wiki/Italian_training_ship_Amerigo_Vespucci" title="Italian training ship Amerigo Vespucci"><i>Amerigo Vespucci</i></a>  in <a href="/wiki/New_York_Harbor" title="New York Harbor">New York Harbor</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3677" data-file-width="5000" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/RMS_Titanic_3.jpg/220px-RMS_Titanic_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/RMS_Titanic_3.jpg/330px-RMS_Titanic_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/RMS_Titanic_3.jpg/440px-RMS_Titanic_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic" title="RMS Titanic">RMS <i>Titanic</i></a> departs from Southampton. Her sinking led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Changes_in_safety_practices_following_the_RMS_Titanic_disaster" title="Changes in safety practices following the RMS Titanic disaster">tighter safety regulations</a></div></div></div>
<p>Parallel to the development of warships, ships in service of marine fishery and trade also developed in the period between antiquity and the Renaissance.
</p><p>Maritime trade was driven by the development of shipping companies with significant financial resources. Canal barges, towed by draft animals on an adjacent <a href="/wiki/Towpath" title="Towpath">towpath</a>, contended with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway" title="Railway">railway</a> up to and past the early days of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a>. Flat-bottomed and flexible <a href="/wiki/Scow" title="Scow">scow</a> boats also became widely used for transporting small cargoes. Mercantile trade went hand-in-hand with exploration, self-financed by the commercial benefits of exploration.
</p><p>During the first half of the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/French_Navy" title="French Navy">French Navy</a> began to develop a new type of vessel known as a <a href="/wiki/Ship_of_the_line" title="Ship of the line">ship of the line</a>, featuring seventy-four guns. This type of ship became the backbone of all European fighting fleets. These ships were 56 metres (184Â ft) long and their construction required 2,800 oak trees and 40 kilometres (25Â mi) of rope; they carried a crew of about 800 sailors and soldiers.
</p><p>During the 19th century the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> enforced a ban on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>, acted to suppress <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>, and continued to map the world. A <a href="/wiki/Clipper" title="Clipper">clipper</a> was a very fast sailing ship of the 19th century. The <a href="/wiki/Clipper_route" title="Clipper route">clipper routes</a> fell into commercial disuse with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steam ships</a> with better fuel efficiency, and the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canals</a>.
</p><p>Ship designs stayed fairly unchanged until the late 19th century. The industrial revolution, new mechanical methods of <a href="/wiki/Propulsion" title="Propulsion">propulsion</a>, and the ability to construct ships from metal triggered an explosion in ship design. Factors including the quest for more efficient ships, the end of long running and wasteful maritime conflicts, and the increased financial capacity of industrial powers created an avalanche of more specialized boats and ships. Ships built for entirely new functions, such as firefighting, rescue, and research, also began to appear.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="581" data-file-width="1748" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg/220px-Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg/330px-Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg/440px-Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colombo.Express.wmt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colombo_Express" title="Colombo Express">Colombo Express</a></i>, one of the largest container ships in the world, owned and operated by <a href="/wiki/Hapag-Lloyd" title="Hapag-Lloyd">Hapag-Lloyd</a> of <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 2019, the world's fleet included 51,684 commercial vessels with <a href="/wiki/Gross_tonnage" title="Gross tonnage">gross tonnage</a> of more than 1,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ton_(volume)" title="Ton (volume)">tons</a>, totaling 1.96Â billion tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>  Such ships carried 11Â billion tons of cargo in 2018, a sum that grew by 2.7% over the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>  In terms of tonnage, 29% of ships were <a href="/wiki/Tanker_(ship)" title="Tanker (ship)">tankers</a>, 43% are <a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">bulk carriers</a>, 13% <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a> and 15% were other types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, there were 1,240 <a href="/wiki/Warship" title="Warship">warships</a> operating in the world, not counting small vessels such as <a href="/wiki/Patrol_boat" title="Patrol boat">patrol boats</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> accounted for 3 million tons worth of these vessels, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> 1.35 million tons, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> 504,660 tons and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> 402,830 tons. The 20th century saw many naval engagements during the two <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">world wars</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, and the rise to power of naval forces of the two blocs. The world's major powers have recently used their naval power in cases such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Falkland_Islands" title="Falkland Islands">Falkland Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.
</p><p>The size of the world's <a href="/wiki/Fishing_fleet" title="Fishing fleet">fishing fleet</a> is more difficult to estimate. The largest of these are counted as commercial vessels, but the smallest are legion. <a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">Fishing vessels</a> can be found in most seaside villages in the world. As of 2004, the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> estimated 4Â million fishing vessels were operating worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao25_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao25-44">[43]</a></sup>  The same study estimated that the world's 29Â million fishermen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> caught 85,800,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (84,400,000 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long tons</a>; 94,600,000 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) of fish and shellfish that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_ships">Types of ships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Types of ships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_types_of_naval_vessels" title="List of types of naval vessels">List of types of naval vessels</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_boat_types" title="List of boat types">List of boat types</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Container_ship.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="144" data-file-width="384" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Container_ship.jpg/220px-Container_ship.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Container_ship.jpg/330px-Container_ship.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5f/Container_ship.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Container_ship.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ship carrying containers in Gadiara (<a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>Because ships are constructed using the principles of naval architecture that require same structural components, their classification is based on their function such as that suggested by Paulet and Presles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> which requires modification of the components. The categories accepted in general by naval architects are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/High-speed_craft" title="High-speed craft">High-speed craft</a> â <a href="/wiki/Multihull" title="Multihull">Multihulls</a> including wave piercers, <a href="/wiki/Small-waterplane-area_twin_hull" title="Small-waterplane-area twin hull">small-waterplane-area twin hull</a> (SWATH), <a href="/wiki/Surface_effect_ship" title="Surface effect ship">surface effect ships</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hovercraft" title="Hovercraft">hovercraft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrofoil" title="Hydrofoil">hydrofoil</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground_effect_(aircraft)" title="Ground effect (aircraft)">wing in ground effect</a> craft (WIG).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Offshore_drilling" title="Offshore drilling">Off shore oil</a> vessels â <a href="/wiki/Platform_supply_vessel" title="Platform supply vessel">Platform supply vessel</a>, pipe layers, accommodation and <a href="/wiki/Crane_vessel" title="Crane vessel">crane</a> <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barges</a>, non and semi-submersible <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rigs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">production platforms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floating_production_storage_and_offloading" title="Floating production storage and offloading">floating production storage and offloading</a> units.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">Fishing vessels</a>
<ul><li>Motorised <a href="/wiki/Fishing_trawler" title="Fishing trawler">fishing trawlers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_traps" title="Fish traps">trap setters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">seiners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Longline_fishing" title="Longline fishing">longliners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trolling_(fishing)" title="Trolling (fishing)">trollers</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Factory_ship" title="Factory ship">factory ships</a>.</li>
<li>Traditional <a href="/wiki/Sailboat" title="Sailboat">sailing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watercraft_rowing" title="Watercraft rowing">rowed</a> fishing vessels and <a href="/wiki/Boat" title="Boat">boats</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Handline_fishing" title="Handline fishing">handline fishing</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harbour" title="Harbour">Harbour</a> work craft
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cable_layer" title="Cable layer">Cable layers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tugboat" title="Tugboat">Tugboats</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dredger" title="Dredger">dredgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_salvage" title="Marine salvage">salvage</a> vessels, <a href="/wiki/Ship%27s_tender" title="Ship's tender">tenders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pilot_boat" title="Pilot boat">Pilot boats</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drydock#Floating" title="Drydock">Floating dry docks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crane_vessel" title="Crane vessel">floating cranes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lighter_aboard_ship" title="Lighter aboard ship">lightership</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>Dry cargo ships â tramp freighters, bulk carriers, cargo liners, container vessels, barge carriers, Ro-Ro ships, refrigerated cargo ships, timber carriers, livestock &amp; light vehicle carriers.</li>
<li>Liquid cargo ships â oil tankers, liquefied gas carriers, chemical carriers.</li>
<li>Passenger vessels
<ul><li>Liners, cruise and special trade passenger (STP) ships</li>
<li>Cross-channel, coastal and harbour ferries</li>
<li>Luxury and cruising yachts</li>
<li>Sail training and multi-masted ships</li></ul></li>
<li>Recreational boats and craft â rowed, masted and motorised craft</li>
<li>Special-purpose vessels â <a href="/wiki/Weather_ship" title="Weather ship">weather</a> and <a href="/wiki/Research_vessel" title="Research vessel">research vessels</a>, deep sea <a href="/wiki/Survey_vessel" title="Survey vessel">survey vessels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a>.</li>
<li>Submersibles â industrial exploration, scientific research, tourist and hydrographic survey.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warships" title="Warships">Warships</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_combatants" title="Surface combatants">surface combatants</a> â <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvettes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minesweeper" title="Minesweeper">minesweepers</a>, etc.</li></ul>
<p>Some of these are discussed in the following sections.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inland_vessels">Inland vessels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Inland vessels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1146" data-file-width="2541" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG/220px-Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG/330px-Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG/440px-Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wappen_von_K%C3%B6ln.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Passenger ship of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ln-D%C3%BCsseldorfer" title="KÃ¶ln-DÃ¼sseldorfer">KÃ¶ln-DÃ¼sseldorfer</a> on the river <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hurma,_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG/220px-Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG/330px-Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG/440px-Hurma%2C_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hurma,_Hans_ja_Voima.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Hurma</i>, <i>Hans</i> and <i>Voima</i> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Saimaa" title="Lake Saimaa">Lake Saimaa</a> in the harbour of <a href="/wiki/Imatra" title="Imatra">Imatra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, at a heritage ship meeting in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>Freshwater shipping may occur on lakes, rivers and canals. Ships designed for those body of waters may be specially adapted to the widths and depths of specific waterways. Examples of freshwater waterways that are navigable in part by large vessels include the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_River" title="Rhine River">Rhine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon</a> Rivers, and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Lakes">Great Lakes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Great Lakes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lake_freighter" title="Lake freighter">Lake freighters</a>, also called lakers, are <a href="/wiki/Cargo" title="Cargo">cargo</a> vessels that ply the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>. The most well-known is <a href="/wiki/SS_Edmund_Fitzgerald" title="SS Edmund Fitzgerald">SSÂ <i>Edmund Fitzgerald</i></a>, the latest major vessel to be wrecked on the Lakes. These vessels are traditionally called boats, not ships. Visiting ocean-going vessels are called "salties". Because of their additional <a href="/wiki/Beam_(nautical)" title="Beam (nautical)">beam</a>, very large salties are never seen inland of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a>. Because the smallest of the <a href="/wiki/Soo_Locks" title="Soo Locks">Soo Locks</a> is larger than any Seaway lock, salties that can pass through the Seaway may travel anywhere in the Great Lakes. Because of their deeper draft, salties may accept partial loads on the Great Lakes, "topping off" when they have exited the Seaway. Similarly, the largest lakers are confined to the Upper Lakes (<a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Superior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Huron" title="Lake Huron">Huron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Erie</a>) because they are too large to use the Seaway locks, beginning at the <a href="/wiki/Welland_Canal" title="Welland Canal">Welland Canal</a> that bypasses the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_River" title="Niagara River">Niagara River</a>.
</p><p>Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> lakes are less corrosive to ships than the <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">salt water</a> of the oceans, lakers tend to last much longer than ocean freighters. Lakers older than 50Â years are not unusual, and as of 2005, all were over 20Â years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/SS_St._Marys_Challenger" title="SS St. Marys Challenger">SSÂ <i>St. Marys Challenger</i></a>, built in 1906 as <i>William P Snyder</i>, was the oldest laker still working on the Lakes until its conversion into a barge starting in 2013. Similarly, <i>E.M. Ford</i>, built in 1898 as <i>Presque Isle</i>, was sailing the lakes 98Â years later in 1996. As of 2007 <i>E.M. Ford</i> was still afloat as a stationary transfer vessel at a riverside cement silo in <a href="/wiki/Saginaw,_Michigan" title="Saginaw, Michigan">Saginaw, Michigan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Merchant_ship">Merchant ship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Merchant ship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Merchant_ship" title="Merchant ship">Merchant ship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Container_ships_President_Truman_(IMO_8616283)_and_President_Kennedy_(IMO_8616295)_at_San_Francisco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="1832" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg/220px-Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg/330px-Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg/440px-Container_ships_President_Truman_%28IMO_8616283%29_and_President_Kennedy_%28IMO_8616295%29_at_San_Francisco.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Container_ships_President_Truman_(IMO_8616283)_and_President_Kennedy_(IMO_8616295)_at_San_Francisco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two modern <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Merchant_ship" title="Merchant ship">Merchant ships</a> are ships used for commercial purposes and can be divided into four broad categories: fishing, <a href="/wiki/Cargo_ship" title="Cargo ship">cargo ships</a>, <a href="/wiki/Passenger_ship" title="Passenger ship">passenger ships</a>, and special-purpose ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNCTAD_review_of_maritime_transport" title="UNCTAD review of maritime transport">UNCTAD review of maritime transport</a> categorizes ships as: oil tankers, bulk (and combination) carriers, general cargo ships, container ships, and "other ships", which includes "<a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gas</a> carriers, <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_natural_gas" title="Liquefied natural gas">liquefied natural gas</a> carriers, parcel (chemical) tankers, specialized tankers, reefers, offshore supply, tugs, dredgers, cruise, ferries, other non-cargo". General cargo ships include "multi-purpose and project vessels and roll-on/roll-off cargo".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNCTAD_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNCTAD-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern commercial vessels are typically powered by a single propeller driven by a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel</a> or, less usually, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_turbine_engine" title="Gas turbine engine">gas turbine engine</a>.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> but until the mid-19th century they were predominantly square sail rigged. The fastest vessels may use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pump-jet_engine" title="Pump-jet engine">pump-jet engines</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Most commercial vessels have full hull-forms to maximize cargo capacity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Hulls are usually made of steel, although aluminum can be used on faster craft, and fiberglass on the smallest service vessels.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>  Commercial vessels generally have a crew headed by a <a href="/wiki/Sea_captain" title="Sea captain">sea captain</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deck_officer" title="Deck officer">deck officers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engine_officer" title="Engine officer">engine officers</a> on larger vessels. Special-purpose vessels often have specialized crew if necessary, for example scientists aboard <a href="/wiki/Research_vessel" title="Research vessel">research vessels</a>.
</p><p>Fishing boats are generally small, often little more than 30 meters (98Â ft) but up to 100 metres (330Â ft) for a large tuna or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whaling_ship" title="Whaling ship">whaling ship</a>. Aboard a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_processing_vessel" title="Fish processing vessel">fish processing vessel</a>, the catch can be made ready for market and sold more quickly once the ship makes port. Special purpose vessels have special gear. For example, trawlers have winches and arms, stern-trawlers have a rear ramp, and tuna seiners have skiffs. In 2004, 85,800,000 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> (84,400,000 <a href="/wiki/Long_ton" title="Long ton">long tons</a>; 94,600,000 <a href="/wiki/Short_ton" title="Short ton">short tons</a>) of fish were caught in the marine capture fishery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao11_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-fao11-52">[51]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchoveta" title="Anchoveta">Anchoveta</a> represented the largest single catch at 10,700,000 tonnes (10,500,000 long tons; 11,800,000 short tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao11_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao11-52">[51]</a></sup>  That year, the top ten marine capture species also included <a href="/wiki/Alaska_pollock" title="Alaska pollock">Alaska pollock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_whiting" title="Blue whiting">Blue whiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skipjack_tuna" title="Skipjack tuna">Skipjack tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">Atlantic herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chub_mackerel" title="Chub mackerel">Chub mackerel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">Japanese anchovy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trachurus" title="Trachurus">Chilean jack mackerel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Largehead_hairtail" title="Largehead hairtail">Largehead hairtail</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yellowfin_tuna" title="Yellowfin tuna">Yellowfin tuna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao11_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-fao11-52">[51]</a></sup>  Other species including <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">shrimp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crab" title="Crab">crab</a>, are also commercially fished. Modern commercial fishermen use many methods. One is fishing by <a href="/wiki/Fishing_net" title="Fishing net">nets</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Seine_fishing" title="Seine fishing">purse seine</a>, beach seine, lift nets, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gillnet" title="Gillnet">gillnets</a>, or entangling nets. Another is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trawl" title="Trawl">trawling</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Bottom_trawling" title="Bottom trawling">bottom trawl</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fish_hook" title="Fish hook">Hooks</a> and lines are used in methods like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-line_fishing" title="Long-line fishing">long-line fishing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hand-line_fishing" title="Hand-line fishing">hand-line fishing</a>. Another method is the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishing_trap" title="Fishing trap">fishing trap</a>.
</p><p>Cargo ships transport dry and liquid cargo. Dry cargo can be transported in bulk by <a href="/wiki/Bulk_carrier" title="Bulk carrier">bulk carriers</a>, packed directly onto a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_cargo_ship" title="General cargo ship">general cargo ship</a> in break-bulk, packed in <a href="/wiki/Intermodal_container" title="Intermodal container">intermodal containers</a> as aboard a <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ship</a>, or driven aboard as in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roll-on_roll-off_ship" title="Roll-on roll-off ship">roll-on roll-off ships</a>. Liquid cargo is generally carried in bulk aboard tankers, such as <a href="/wiki/Oil_tanker" title="Oil tanker">oil tankers</a> which may include both crude and finished products of oil, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_tanker" title="Chemical tanker">chemical tankers</a> which may also carry vegetable oils other than chemicals and <a href="/wiki/Gas_carrier" title="Gas carrier">gas carriers</a>, although smaller shipments may be carried on container ships in <a href="/wiki/Tank_container" title="Tank container">tank containers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gubbins_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gubbins-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Passenger_ship" title="Passenger ship">Passenger ships</a> range in size from small river ferries to very large <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a>. This type of vessel includes <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">ferries</a>, which move passengers and vehicles on short trips; <a href="/wiki/Ocean_liner" title="Ocean liner">ocean liners</a>, which carry passengers from one place to another; and <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ships</a>, which carry passengers on voyages undertaken for pleasure, visiting several places and with leisure activities on board, often returning them to the port of embarkation. <a href="/wiki/Riverboat" title="Riverboat">Riverboats</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferry_boat" title="Ferry boat">inland ferries</a> are specially designed to carry passengers, cargo, or both in the challenging river environment. Rivers present special hazards to vessels. They usually have varying water flows that alternately lead to high speed water flows or protruding rock hazards. Changing siltation patterns may cause the sudden appearance of shoal waters, and often floating or sunken logs and trees (called snags) can endanger the hulls and propulsion of riverboats. Riverboats are generally of shallow draft, being broad of beam and rather square in plan, with a low freeboard and high topsides. Riverboats can survive with this type of configuration as they do not have to withstand the high winds or large waves that are seen on large lakes, seas, or oceans.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albatun_Dod.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="921" data-file-width="1228" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Albatun_Dod.jpg/220px-Albatun_Dod.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Albatun_Dod.jpg/330px-Albatun_Dod.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Albatun_Dod.jpg/440px-Albatun_Dod.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albatun_Dod.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Albatun Dos</i>, a tuna boat at work near <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Seychelles" title="Victoria, Seychelles">Victoria, Seychelles</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">Fishing vessels</a> are a subset of commercial vessels, but generally small in size and often subject to different regulations and classification. They can be categorized by several criteria: architecture, the type of fish they catch, the fishing method used, geographical origin, and technical features such as rigging. As of 2004, the world's fishing fleet consisted of some 4 million vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao25_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-fao25-44">[43]</a></sup>  Of these, 1.3 million were decked vessels with enclosed areas and the rest were open vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao25_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-fao25-44">[43]</a></sup>  Most decked vessels were mechanized, but two-thirds of the open vessels were traditional craft propelled by sails and oars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fao25_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-fao25-44">[43]</a></sup>  More than 60% of all existing large fishing vessels<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[note 2]</a></sup> were built in Japan, Peru, the Russian Federation, Spain or the United States of America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_purpose_vessels">Special purpose vessels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ship&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Special purpose vessels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Weather_ship" title="Weather ship">Weather ship</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarfront.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1453" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarfront.jpg/220px-Polarfront.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarfront.jpg/330px-Polarfront.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Polarfront.jpg/440px-Polarfront.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarfront.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The weather ship <a href="/wiki/MS_Polarfront" title="MS Polarfront">MS <i>Polarfront</i></a> at sea.</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Weather_ship" title="Weather ship">weather ship</a> was a ship stationed in the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> as a platform for surface and upper air meteorological observations for use in <a href="/wiki/Marine_weather_forecasting" title="Marine weather forecasting">marine weather forecasting</a>. Surface weather observations were taken hourly, and four radiosonde releases occurred daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cg_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-cg-56">[54]</a></sup>  It was also meant to aid in search and rescue operations and to support transatlantic flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cg_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-cg-56">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-pop-57">[55]</a></sup>  Proposed as early as 1927 by the <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">aviation</a> community,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> the establishment of weather ships proved to be so useful during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> that the <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a> (ICAO) established a global network of weather ships in 1948, with 13 to be supplied by the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-pop-57">[55]</a></sup> This number was eventually negotiated down to nine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roll_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roll-59">[57]</a></sup>
